    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2824b755c8ad435e8566188b1ed2aea1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1b0df349-27eb-448d-ad7a-eb3d4ce76d3a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="epub-sections"><div class="epub-section"><span class="epub-state">First published: </span><span class="epub-date">09 January 2024</span></div>

Transmission to Achieve a Long-Term Stable Sodium Metal Battery Under Harsh Conditions link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202315309">https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202315309</a></div><div    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d229972850" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium metal batteries have emerged as potential rivals to lithium-ion batteries. Nevertheless, maintaining a stable sodium metal anode under harsh conditions (current density &gt;10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) is extremely challenging. The primary issue is the highly reactive sodium metal continuously reacts with the electrolyte to form a thick and loose solid electrolyte film. The tip effect causes sodium ions (Na<sup>+</sup>) accumulated in the bulge resulting in uneven deposition. In this paper, a unique artificial interfacial layer (Ag nanoparticles distributed in NaClO<sub>4</sub>-based matrix) is prepared on the surface of a metallic sodium anode using in situ displacement reaction. Ag particles arranged at the bottom of the artificial layer act as effective nucleation sites helping Na<sup>+</sup> deposit uniformly, while the built-in electric field formed between Ag in the middle/top of the artificial layer and the sodium accelerates the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the interfacial layer during the deposition and reduce the polarization. Such important properties further allow the steady operation of modified sodium metal batteries in very harsh conditions. The symmetric battery achieves reversible stripping/plating for over 1000&nbsp;h under high current density (10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>). Moreover, the full cell coupled with a high-loading Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode (20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) presents a stable cycle performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315309-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The demand for renewable energy storage and electric vehicles has grown exponentially in recent years, thus leading to a further requirement for new battery systems beyond prevailing lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0001_R_d229972744e377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Semicond.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among those alternative battery systems, sodium metal batteries (SMBs) have shown great competitiveness due to their high theoretical specific capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), low redox potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs&nbsp;standard hydrogen electrode) as well as the rich resource of sodium (Na).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0002_R_d229972744e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Rev. Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the inherent instability of Na metal anode contributes to the uncurbed formation of an uneven and delicate solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on Na metal surface during cycling. The unstable SEI causes the irregular transport of Na ions, finally resulting in accumulated dead Na and dendritic growth of Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0003_R_d229972744e390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev. Small Nat. Commun.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the volume expansion of Na anode gives rise to cracks in the fragile SEI, and thus excess electrolyte are consumed to fix them, which intensifies the instability of the interface. These related questions bring about a decline of energy density, poor coulombic efficiencies, a shortened cycling life, and even give rise to short circuit and serious safety issues caused by Na dendrites piercing the separator.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0004_R_d229972744e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nat. Commun.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Many efforts have been made to overcome aforesaid issues, such as constructing a 3D current collector,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0005_R_d229972744e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Ecomat Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Rare Met. J. Semicond.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> introducing new electrolyte systems,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0006_R_d229972744e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem J. Feng, ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Nano Res.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> applying solid-state electrolyte (SSE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0007_R_d229972744e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and designing artificial interface layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0008_R_d229972744e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Building a 3D current collector can reduce the current density and evade the formation of Na dendrite through its 3D structure. Meanwhile, a well-designed current collector can induce the formation of a stable SEI layer as well as uniform Na deposition. Yet, it is not suitable for large-scale production because of its complicated fabrication and high cost. As for liquid electrolytes, although various electrolyte systems have been investigated and tested, the spontaneous interaction between electrolyte and anode cannot be eliminated. Solid-state batteries are safer, but SSE has a low ionic conductivity and poor compatibility with electrodes, making its electrochemical performance unsatisfactory.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0009_R_d229972744e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Technol.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Designing an artificial interface layer with different sodiophilic compounds, metals (Au, Ag, and Sn), or alloys can prevent direct contact between electrolyte and electrode and induce homogeneous initial Na deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0010_R_d229972744e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett. Chem. Eng. J. Small">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, many modification methods such as atomic layer deposition (ALD), and chemical and electrochemical reactions<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0011_R_d229972744e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to build an organic–inorganic composite interface layer are complicated. What's more, the reported artificial interface layer almost cannot achieve a long-term stable Na anode under very harsh conditions (current density &gt; 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) and also cannot achieve SMBs with a high-loading cathode (&gt;10&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0008_R_d229972744e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0012_R_d229972744e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Stor. Mater. Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which seriously hinders its commercial application. The most fundamental reason is that the activity of Na metal anode leads to an unstable and uneven SEI when a full cell works. Afterward, Na dendritic appears and volume exchange occurs, leading to a massive loss of capacity. The situation gets worse when an unstable anode is matched to a cathode with a high mass loading. Therefore, developing a universal strategy to construct a stable interface layer on the Na anode is critical to realizing high-performance SMBs.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we develop an artificial interface layer with sound stability by a simple in situ replacement reaction of Na anode and AgClO<sub>4</sub> in an organic solvent that dissolves AgClO<sub>4</sub> and is stable with metallic Na. Within the above solution, the reaction (Na + AgClO<sub>4</sub> = Ag + NaClO<sub>4</sub>) proceeded to build an anode with an interface layer containing a NaClO<sub>4</sub>-based layer and Ag particles (NClA), as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0001">1a</a>. By controlling the time of soaking, the thickness of the interface layer is regulated, which allows us to study the relationship between its properties and thickness. Then, these anodes were placed in a muffle furnace for heat treatment (110 °C, 30&nbsp;mins) to enhance their stability by further connecting Na metal to the interface layer. The unique protective layer allows for uniform and rapid sodium-ions (Na<sup>+</sup>) transmission as well as stable SEI formation on Na metal during cycling.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315309-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/51bc5da1-63f1-4c3b-af39-0a03f080f1ea/adfm202315309-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/51bc5da1-63f1-4c3b-af39-0a03f080f1ea/adfm202315309-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bb325ad7-e1f2-4ce5-8027-0de8949c8ae8/adfm202315309-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/51bc5da1-63f1-4c3b-af39-0a03f080f1ea/adfm202315309-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315309-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315309" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustrations, physicochemical characterizations, and surface observation of NClA. a) Schematic showing the synthesis process of the NClA. b) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of NClA and bare Na. c) Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) spectra of NClA and NaClO<sub>4</sub> sample. d) XPS Ag 3<i>d</i> spectrum of NClA. e) XPS Cl 2<i>p</i> spectrum of NClA. f) Surface photographs and scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of NClA anodes after heat treatment. g) EDS mapping of for Ag, Cl, Na, and O of the SEM image. h) Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image and Fourier Transform Algorithm images of selected area.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The uniform Ag-rich sodiophilic layer located at the bottom of the artificial layer can significantly reduce the nucleation overpotential and promote the uniform deposition of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0010_R_d229972744e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett. Chem. Eng. J. Small">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0013_R_d229972744e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nano-Micro Lett. Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, due to the higher electrode potential of Ag (0.7991&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode) relative to Na, the natural built-in electric field between the Ag in the middle/top of the artificial layer and the Na at the bottom will accelerate the movement of Na<sup>+</sup> in the interfacial layer during deposition, which accelerates interfacial transport dynamics and reduce internal battery polarization. The mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> deposition in the modified symmetric cell is further investigated through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and atomic force microscope (AFM), proving that the Na<sup>+</sup> are induced to be evenly deposited in the Na metal base after going through the prepared protective layer. As a result, an adjustable solid interface layer enables long-term lifespan cycling in symmetric cells with high current density (over 1100&nbsp;h at 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>), over 800 cycles of life in full cells with low loading active mass cathode (∼ 2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, 1 C) and over 99% capacity retention after 50 cycles in full cells with high loading active mass cathode (20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, 0.3 C).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315309-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The adjustable and facile procedure to prepare the interface layer on the surface of Na metal can be attributed to the in situ reduction of AgClO<sub>4</sub> by Na at room temperature:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202315309-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7236bf4c-ef76-49db-8a75-96e3effe7638/adfm202315309-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202315309:adfm202315309-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202315309-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi>AgCl</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mi>NaCl</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mrow>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi>Ag</mi>
                           </mrow>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{Na + AgCl}}{{\mathrm{O}}}_{\mathrm{4}}{\mathrm{ = NaCl}}{{\mathrm{O}}}_{\mathrm{4}}{\mathrm{ + Ag}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The kinetics of Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202315309-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>) are slow because the in situ reduction occurs in an organic solvent where AgClO<sub>4</sub> is slightly soluble. The X-ray diffraction (XRD)&nbsp;spectrum of NClA shows similar characteristic peaks with Ag PDF and the XRD spectrum of pure Na, indicating the existence of Ag crystal on the surface of anode before and after the heat treatment (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0001">1b</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0001">1c</a> shows that the Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) spectra of both NClA and NaClO<sub>4</sub> have a similar spectrum between 1500 and 500 cm<sup>−1</sup> in which peak at 1100 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be ascribed to Cl─O stretching vibration, peaks at 650 and 610–620&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> can be ascribed to ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> contact ion pair and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> free anions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0014_R_d229972744e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Polym. Sci., Part A: Polym. Chem.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is also well worth noticing that the XPS spectra of NClA further prove the presence and valence state of Ag and Cl elements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0001">1d,e</a>). The Ag 3<i>d</i> spectrum exhibits main peaks at 368.2 and 374.5&nbsp;eV which can be assigned to Ag 3<i>d</i><sub>3/2</sub> (metal) and Ag 3<i>d</i><sub>5/2</sub> (metal). While each chemical state of Cl element has two spin–orbit split peaks, the peaks at 210.3 and 208.6&nbsp;eV in the Cl 2<i>p</i> spectrum are attributed to Cl 2<i>p</i> (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>), and the peaks at 200.15&nbsp;eV and 198.5&nbsp;eV stand for Cl 2<i>p</i> (Cl<sup>−</sup>). In addition, a small number of ClO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> are also shown (206.5 and 207.6&nbsp;eV), which come from the commercial AgClO<sub>4</sub> and the reduced ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> during the preparation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0015_R_d229972744e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Sci. Eur. Polym. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) shows the XPS spectrum of pure AgClO<sub>4</sub>, which exhibits similar peaks compared with that of the NClA anode, further indicating the existence of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>. Through XRD, FTIR, and XPS, we further indicate that Ag metal and NaClO<sub>4</sub> occupy the majority of interface layer. As the majority of the interface layer, NaClO<sub>4</sub>, widely used as part of the electrolyte in the synthesis of solid-state sodium-ion batteries and SMBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0015_R_d229972744e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Sci. Eur. Polym. J.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0016_R_d229972744e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> has favourable ion conduction properties.</p>
         
         <p>Apart from the composition of the interface layer fabricated by in situ reduction, we further investigated the morphology of the interface layer (obtained by reaction for 2&nbsp;h) and the distribution of the different components. Since there existed cracks on the modified interface layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">1f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the heat treatment (110 °C, 30 mins) was used to enhance its flatness because the molten state Na metal better combined with the interface layer at a temperature of 110 °C near the melting point of Na metal. The thicknesses of the artificial interface layer on Na anode can be regulated by adjusting the time it soaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The thickness of the artificially modified layer for 2&nbsp;h immersion is about 20&nbsp;µm, while for 8&nbsp;h is 70&nbsp;µm. The distribution of Ag and Cl elements on the surface and cross-section was further explored by EDS mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), and the reduced metallic Ag existed in the form of nano-particles and was evenly distributed in the interface layer. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0001">1h</a> shows the Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image of the interface layer, where the selected fuscous area showed the same lattice spacing of Ag crystal according to standard Ag PDF. These areas exhibited various lattice spacing (d = 0.2457, 0.2412, 0.2126&nbsp;nm) after Fourier transform algorithm which can be directly attributed to the (200) and (111) crystal orientations of Ag. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) further exhibits EDS images of the Cl, Na, O, and Ag elements of the TEM images. In general, an artificial interface layer with about 20&nbsp;µm thickness dominated by NaClO<sub>4</sub>/Ag nanoparticles was obtained. The longitudinal distribution of Ag metal particles in the interface layer can act as sodiophilic seeds and assist in the construction of internal electric fields, thus achieving uniform and rapid Na deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0013_R_d229972744e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nano-Micro Lett. Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0017_R_d229972744e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater. Small Methods ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To further explore the effect of the interface layer on the electrochemical stability of the Na metal anode and its improvements to deposition and stripping, the assembled symmetric battery was tested in charge–discharge cycles. When tested at a current density of 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0002">2a</a>), the NClA||NClA symmetrical cell has a smooth and stable time–voltage curve, maintaining an overpotential of 70–80&nbsp;mV at the beginning and indicating a stable protective interface layer as well as rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport with little pernicious electrochemical reaction. The overvoltage decreases as the cycling time goes, for a stable SEI forms as the charge/discharge process goes. The NClA||NClA symmetrical cell exhibits excellent cycling stability for over 1000&nbsp;h, which can be largely attributed to the unique physical and chemical properties of the artificial interface layer. First, NaClO<sub>4</sub> with the electron nonconductive and ion conduction property prevents the Na metal deposition on the surface of the artificial interface layer but under it. Secondly, Ag particles in contact with Na metal at the bottom of the interface layer can act as implanting sodiophilic seeds and induce uniform nucleation and deposition of Na metal. Thirdly, the Ag particles in the middle/top of the interfacial layer and the Na metal at the bottom can form a built-in electric field, accelerating the transport of Ag particles in the interfacial layer and reducing polarization due to charge accumulation on the surface of the electrode. In contrast, under the same current density (10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), the overvoltage of bare Na symmetrical cell in the comparison sample is unstable and as high as 130&nbsp;mV, rising continually to 150&nbsp;mV. Its overvoltage fluctuates greatly during the cycle, and the overvoltage drops sharply after cycling for 110&nbsp;h, indicating the battery fails to work due to a short circuit. Compared with the other top journals,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0008_R_d229972744e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0012_R_d229972744e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Stor. Mater. Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0018_R_d229972744e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the stability of our assembled NClA||NClA symmetric battery shows comparable and even better stability at the same or similar current density (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0002">2b</a>). By the way, at the beginning of the long cycling, the symmetric cells were tested from 0.5 to 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, and each stage lasted for 5 cycles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0002">2c</a>. The apparent activation energy (E<sub>a</sub>) of the modified layer in NClA and SEI layer in cycled Bare Na and NClA (10th&nbsp;cycling under 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>-2</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0002">2d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the Nyquist plots of the Na||Na and NClA||NClA cells at different temperatures (283–308 K), the apparent activation energy (E<sub>a</sub>) of the modified layer of original NClA is calculated according to the Arrhenius equation is 26.35&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>. After the 10th cycling under 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>-2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>-2</sup>, the E<sub>a</sub> of the SEI in the NClA slightly increases to 29.26&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>-1</sup> because the diglyme and NaPF<sub>6</sub> in electrolyte also involved in the formation of SEI. However, the E<sub>a</sub> of the SEI in cycled NClA is still lower than that of in cycled Bare Na electrode (35.06&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>), which proves that Na<sup>+</sup> can pass through the modified artificial layer more easily. This is because the natural built-in electric field between Ag in the artificial layer and Na at the bottom of the artificial layer accelerates the transmission of Na<sup>+</sup> in the interfacial layer during deposition. As Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0002">2e</a> summarized, while the NaClO<sub>4</sub>-base layer helps build a well-stabilized SEI, the sodiophilic Ag particles induce the deposition behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>. Their combination maintains stable and uniform Na<sup>+</sup> transport and deposition under the interface layer during the process of charging/discharging. On the contrary, the flow of Na<sup>+</sup> accumulates at the bumps on the surface of Bare Na anode resulting in uneven deposition.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315309-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/01f36576-aae6-4b41-a9f7-75888e7cd9e4/adfm202315309-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/01f36576-aae6-4b41-a9f7-75888e7cd9e4/adfm202315309-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/abc196fb-a847-47e3-9aa5-4dc46bdd5546/adfm202315309-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/01f36576-aae6-4b41-a9f7-75888e7cd9e4/adfm202315309-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315309-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315309" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the symmetric cell and Na plating/stripping behavior. a) Cycling performance of NClA||NClA and Bare Na||Bare Na under current density of 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) Comparation of our work with other research in their performance of symmetric cell. c) Cycling performance of NClA||NClA and Bare Na||Bare Na under current density from 0.5 to 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. d) Activation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> across the original artificial layer on the fresh NClA and SEI layer on the cycled Bare Na and NClA after 10 cycles under 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The results were derived from the Nyquist plots and Arrhenius equation. e)Schematic illustration of the protection mechanism of NClA. f) EIS image of NClA||NClA and Na||Na after 1, 10, 30, and 50 cycles under 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. g,h) Na<sup>+</sup> transport number testing of NClA||NClA and Bare Na||Bare Na.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To study the influence of the modified layer on the impedance of the symmetric cells, we employed Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) to the NClA||NClA and Bare Na||Bare Na after 1, 10, 30, 50 cycles under 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0002">2f</a>). To compare the impedance of NClA||NClA and Bare Na||Bare Na cells in various dimensions, the impedance of electrode and interface (R<sub>s</sub>), the impedance of SEI (R<sub>SEI</sub>) and Charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) were calculated and summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). As cycle number increases, <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> of both symmetric cells increase. The R<sub>s</sub> of Bare Na||Bare Na rises sharply (from 4.445 to 8.007 Ω) while that of NClA||NClA fluctuates little (from 5.735 to 6.105 Ω), indicating the continuous side reactions between the electrolyte and the Na metal surface during cycling and the continuous buildup of broken SEI. Besides, the values of R<sub>SEI</sub> and R<sub>ct</sub> of NClA remain stable after the 10th, 30th, and 50th cycle (from 3.523 to 4.02 Ω), while those of the Bare Na symmetric batteries constantly increase (from 2.866 to 7.607 Ω), demonstrating that NClA has a more stable SEI layer and faster electrochemical reaction compared to the Bare Na. Since the SEI formed spontaneously on the Bare Na during cycling is inhomogeneous thus leads to a low ionic conductivity and electrochemical reaction rate. In contrast, the artificial interfacial layer on the NClA effectively induces the uniform deposition of Na<sup>+</sup>, accelerating electrochemical reaction kinetics. To further examine the influence of the protection layer on Na<sup>+</sup> transport, the Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (t<sub>Na+</sub>) was carried out. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0002">2g,h</a>, the Na<sup>+</sup> migration number of NClA is 0.7489, which is much higher than that of Bare Na metal anode (0.4783). Their discrepancy in Na<sup>+</sup> transport number could be attributed to the difference in penetration ability in interface layer. While Bare Na enables the penetration of all cations and anions, the NClA anode has an artificial interface layer that only allows the penetration of Na<sup>+</sup>. Such peculiarity of the interface layer further benefits an orderly deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> through superficial SEI.</p>
         
         <p>XPS depth profile (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0003">3</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) was also employed to study the species and longitudinal distribution of components in the SEI layer on the cycled Bare Na and NClA anodes. The Ag 3<i>d</i> spectra of NClA||NClA indicate the amount of Ag metal (368.3&nbsp;eV, 374.5&nbsp;eV) is almost unchanged as etch time goes, which means that Ag nanoparticles are uniformly distributed in the SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0019_R_d229972744e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Surf. Sci.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Cl 2<i>p</i> spectra display two components, NaClO<sub>4</sub> (210.1 and 208.9&nbsp;eV) and NaCl (201.0 and 199.6&nbsp;eV). The NaClO<sub>4</sub> in the artificial interface layer is partially dissolved in the electrolyte during cycling, which is consistent with the FTIR spectra of NClA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). However, the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>-</sup> is not detected after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching, which may be due to the decomposition of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>-</sup> by high-energy Ar<sup>+</sup>. The XPS depth profile of AgClO<sub>4</sub> (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information) revealed that, with high-energy Ar<sup>+</sup> etching, the peaks of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>-</sup> were significantly decreased, and most of them were converted to ClO<sub>3</sub><sup>-</sup> and Cl<sup>-</sup>. More importantly, NaCl, which is formed by the reduction of dissolved ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> and NaClO<sub>4</sub> left on the NClA surface, is highly insoluble in the organic electrolyte and participates in the composition of a stable SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0020_R_d229972744e933" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Cl<sup>−</sup> existed in the SEI of cycled Bare Na is due to the trace contaminants introduced during the sample preparation process. F 1<i>s</i> exhibits peaks of Na─F (683.9&nbsp;eV) and P─F (687.45&nbsp;eV), which can be attributed to the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> during cycling. For P 2<i>p</i> spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), peaks at 137.7 and 137.0&nbsp;eV can be attributed to PO<sub>x</sub>F<sub>y</sub>, peaks at 132.8 and 133.6&nbsp;eV can be ascribed to PO<sup>3−</sup>, and the other peaks can be ascribed to PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> (132.4 and 133.3&nbsp;eV). It is further confirmed that PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> decomposition provides an inorganic-rich interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0021_R_d229972744e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Chem. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C 1<i>s</i> spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) of both anodes are similar, peaks at 289.1, 286.7, and 284.8&nbsp;eV can be attributed to O─C═O, C─O, C─C/C─H, respectively, originating from decomposition of diglyme.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0022_R_d229972744e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, C-containing species of NClA can be found to decline more rapidly with increasing depth, which means the artificial layer with NaClO<sub>4</sub> and Ag inhibits the decomposition of diglyme. O 1<i>s</i> spectra exhibit peaks of C─O/C═O (531.9&nbsp;eV) and Na─O (530.9&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0023_R_d229972744e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the peak at 535.4&nbsp;eV in O 1<i>s</i> spectra of cycled NClA also confirms the existence of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>. Therefore, the artificial layer containing NaClO<sub>4</sub> and Ag with Built-in electric field and efficient nucleation sites enables rapid migration and uniform deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> and thus reduces the polarization and decomposition of electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315309-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/44826450-b7b5-45da-80ba-3093c33c0e66/adfm202315309-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/44826450-b7b5-45da-80ba-3093c33c0e66/adfm202315309-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2337e980-4266-4ed3-89d1-7d7d1e01e993/adfm202315309-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/44826450-b7b5-45da-80ba-3093c33c0e66/adfm202315309-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315309-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315309" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS depth profile of NClA||NClA and Bare Na||Bare Na anode after 100&nbsp;h of cycling under 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. a) XPS Ag 3<i>d</i>, Cl 2<i>p</i>, O 1<i>s</i>, F 1<i>s</i> spectra of NClA||NClA (sorted from left to right). b) XPS Ag 3<i>d</i>, Cl 2<i>p</i>, O 1<i>s</i>, F 1<i>s</i> spectra of Bare Na||Bare Na (sorted from left to right). Vertical coordinates of the same elements are adjusted equally.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To better understand the behavior of the Na deposition and stripping, AFM is applied to investigate the Young's modulus maps and the force-displacement curves of Bare Na and NClA before and after cycling. NClA exhibits a higher Young's modulus compared to Bare Na (2733&nbsp;MPa vs 267&nbsp;MPa), which is beneficial to the formation of a stronger SEI layer and helps to resist the huge volume change by the subsequent cycling process. During the subsequent deposition and stripping processes, Bare Na and NCIA show the same trend with an increase in Young's modulus on the deposition side and a further increase after stripping. The average values of Young's modulus for the Deposited Bare Na, Stripped Bare Na, Deposited NClA, and Stripped NClA are 973, 13&nbsp;359, 4934, and 28&nbsp;885&nbsp;MPa, respectively (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0004">4a–f</a>). The rise of the modulus of deposited Bare Na and NClA can be attributed to the formation of SEI. The ultra-high Young's modulus of the deposited side and stripped side can be attributed to the residual exposed Na salts corresponding to the white part in the modulus graph. The stripped side has a more uneven surface relative to the deposited side, resulting in more Na salts that cannot be washed away during the sample preparation, leading to a locally higher Young's modulus. Moreover, a certain amount of deposited Na infiltrates the SEI on the deposition side reducing its modulus. The modulus of the stripped side of NClA is only 5.8 times higher than that of the deposited side, whereas, in the case of the Bare Na, the value is 13.7 times, which proves that more Na penetrates the SEI in the deposited side of Bare Na, resulting in a significant decrease in the modulus of the SEI layer. What's more, the Young's modulus of the electrodes are also obtained by analysing the Force-displacement curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0004">4g,h</a>), which are analogous as the result above. Meanwhile, the NClA and deposited NClA have a much lower difference between the extent and retract curve than that of the bare Na, showing that the NClA electrodes exhibit a higher degree of adhesive elasticity, which further indicates the NClA can provide a more solid surface and thus lead to an even and stable SEI even after multiple cycles under large current density with an elastic layer. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0004">4i,j</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) also exhibits height maps of them, where we can see the deposited NClA has a flatter surface with a lower root mean square height (Rq) compared with that of the bare Na.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315309-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c4e9f75c-0e16-4bfb-82df-48a398aa8c49/adfm202315309-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c4e9f75c-0e16-4bfb-82df-48a398aa8c49/adfm202315309-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8962852c-2038-4ada-abe7-ee2d3ae8eb6e/adfm202315309-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c4e9f75c-0e16-4bfb-82df-48a398aa8c49/adfm202315309-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315309-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315309" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Micromorphology and SEI properties of Bare Na, NClA and that of them as electrodes in symmetric cell after 100&nbsp;h cycling under 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. a–f)Young's modulus maps of (a) Bare Na, (b) Deposited Bare Na, (c) Stripped Bare Na, (d) NClA. (e) Deposited NClA, (f) Stripped NClA. g,h) Force-displacement curves of Bare Na, NClA, Deposited Bare Na, Deposited NClA. (i), (j) height maps of deposited Bare Na and deposited NClA. Their Rq are shown on the top right corner of their graph. k,l) SEM images of NClA and Bare Na anode after cycling for 100&nbsp;h under 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As the surface morphology of Na anode is vital in measuring the stability and mechanism of SMBs, SEM measurement was employed to NClA||NClA and Bare Na||Bare Na (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0004">4k,l</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) after cycling for 10, 50, 100&nbsp;h. After 10&nbsp;h, both the modified anode and bare anode show a similar surface where Na deposition nucleates as regular globules. After 50&nbsp;h, protrusions nucleate and cracks occur on the Na electrode. Protrusions on Na electrode aggregate to form dendrites as well as irregular globules along the cracks at the 100&nbsp;h, implying a possibility of short circuit and undermining the stability of the symmetric battery. In contrast, the anode with modified layer protection exhibits steady plating/stripping of Na<sup>+</sup> with an increase of uniform Na deposition.</p>
         
         <p>To explore the feasibility of modified Na metal in practical application, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathodes with different mass loading (~2 and 20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) were used to match with the NClA anodes to assemble full cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0005">5a,b</a> provides corresponding voltage profiles of the NClA||NVP and Bare Na||NVP cells. In addition, NClA||NVP shows lower polarization and higher capacity retention. The EIS image of the NClA||NVP and Bare Na||NVP cells after certain cycles is further given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0005">5c</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information), and their interface impedance (R<sub>s</sub>) and the Warburg impedance (Z<sub>w</sub>) are calculated in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). Both the R<sub>s</sub> and Z<sub>w</sub> of two full cells increase as cycle goes when that of NClA||NVP increases more slowly, indicating the protective layer enables a lower interface impedance and diffusion impedance under the same circumstances. Rate performance of Bare Na||NVP and NClA||NVP full cells are also shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0005">5d</a>, where NClA||NVP consistently shows higher capacity and coulombic efficiency under different current densities. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0005">5e</a> shows the cycling performance of the NClA||NVP with a mass loading of ∼ 2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. In the first cycle, NVP delivered a capacity of 116 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a high coulombic efficiency of 94%. It also maintains a coulombic efficiency of more than 99% for up to 800 cycles, demonstrating its excellent rate capabilities. In contrast, Bare Na||NVP only worked for about 230 cycles with a rapid decline in coulombic efficiency after 240 cycles. When assembled with an NVP anode with a mass loading of 20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, the NClA||NVP cell also exhibits an excellent capacity of 112 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and coulombic efficiency of 97.5% at the beginning and the capacity retention is 97% after 53 cycles, while the Bare Na||NVP fails after 3 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0005">5f</a>). It confirms the critical role of a protective layer on the NClA in stable SMB operation. These outstanding results outperform cells in many previous reported articles,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0005_R_d229972744e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Ecomat Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Rare Met. J. Semicond.">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0012_R_d229972744e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Stor. Mater. Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202315309-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315309-bib-0024_R_d229972744e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> when only a few articles can succeed in testing full cells with cathode under such a high mass loading (more than 10&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315309-fig-0005">5g</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315309-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8eccd804-976a-4d39-9247-6badd99060f7/adfm202315309-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8eccd804-976a-4d39-9247-6badd99060f7/adfm202315309-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b0bafcf0-2f6c-4cc7-b859-0d62246011df/adfm202315309-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8eccd804-976a-4d39-9247-6badd99060f7/adfm202315309-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315309-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315309" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical test of NClA||NVP and Bare Na||NVP. a,b) Corresponding voltage profiles of the NClA||NVP and Bare Na||NVP cells. c) EIS of the NClA||NVP and Bare Na||NVP cells after 1, 10, 30, and 50 cycles with cathode loading mass of ≈2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. d) Rate performance of Bare Na||NVP and NClA||NVP full cells with cathode loading mass of ∼ 2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. e,f) Cycling stability of Bare Na||NVP and NClA||NVP full cells with cathode loading mass of (e) ≈2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> under 1 C and (f) 20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> under 0.3 C. g) Comparison of our work with other research in their performance of full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315309-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we offered a facile strategy to prepare a unique artificial interface layer on the surface of Na metal anode by an in situ reduction reaction, which consists of NaClO<sub>4</sub> dotted with Ag particles. The obtained NClA anode not only shows excellent stability in the symmetric cell (cycles over 1000&nbsp;h at 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), but also exhibits a long cycle life (&gt; 800 cycles) and remarkable capacity retention in the full cell (50 cycles at 0.3 C) under an ultra-high mass loading (20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>). Through various characterization methods, we studied the mechanism of artificial interface layer to improve the performance of SMBs. The longitudinally dispersed Ag nanoparticles in the artificial layer form a built-in electric field with Na metal, accelerating the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the interfacial layer during deposition and accelerating the interfacial diffusion kinetics. Furthermore, NaClO<sub>4</sub> with high Na<sup>+</sup> mobility and Ag particles at the bottom of the interfacial layer, which are in close contact with Na metal, help Na<sup>+</sup> to nucleate uniformly, thus accelerating and homogenizing the deposition of Na<sup>+</sup>. This strategy paves an innovative and novel way to perform a stable SMBs under large current density and high mass loading, promoting its practical application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315309-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315309-sec-0070-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>Na cubes (≥99.7%) stored in mineral oil were purchased from Aladdin Co., Ltd. Electrolyte (NP-005, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DIGLYME) was purchased from Suzhou Duo Duo Co., Ltd. Polypropylene membrane was purchased from Celgard Co., Ltd. Cathode (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) with a loading of 2 and 20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> was purchased from Guangdong Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd. NaClO<sub>4</sub> (≥99%) was purchased from Aladdin Co., Ltd. AgClO<sub>4</sub>&nbsp;(≥97%) was purchased from MACKLIN Co., Ltd.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315309-sec-0080-title"> Preparation of the Modified Artificial Interface Layer</h4>
                  
                  <p>First, Na block was extruded into Na foil, then a cut-off knife was applied to it to prepare Na anodes with a diameter of 10&nbsp;mm. The as-obtained anodes and superfluous AgClO<sub>4</sub>&nbsp;(30–40&nbsp;mg) were placed in the glass vessel with an amount of organic solvent (40–60&nbsp;mL). AgClO<sub>4</sub> was placed excessively for it is slightly soluble in organic solvent, and such mass of AgClO<sub>4</sub> can guarantee the Ag<sup>+</sup> and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> in the solution remain saturated. After the specified soaking time, the Na anodes were taken out and washed with fresh orgnic solvent. Finally, the obtained Na anodes were put between copper foils in a glass dish, which was then annealed at 110 °C for 30&nbsp;mins with a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> at a muffle furnace. The whole preparation process took place in glovebox (Mikrouna) with the protection of high purity argon gas.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315309-sec-0090-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed on a D-MAX 2200 VPC X-ray diffractometer at a scan rate of 5° min<sup>−1</sup>. The morphology of the samples was studied using scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Regulus 8230, Hitachi Co., Ltd.) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, FEI Tecnai G2 F30, Philips-FEI). The valence state on the surface of the samples was investigated using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, ESCALAB 250, Thermo-VG Scientific Co., Ltd.). Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (ATR-FTIR, Frontier, PerkinElmer) was also used with wavelength ranges from 500 to 4000 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Atomic force microscope (AFM) tests were performed using Bruker Dimension Icon.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315309-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315309-sec-0100-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>All Electrochemical measurements and assembly of cells were carried out with CR2032-type coin cells assembled in a glove box in the atmosphere of argon gas. The symmetric cells were assembled using prepared anodes with 50&nbsp;µL of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DIGLYME as the electrolyte and Polypropylene as the separator.</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) (0.1&nbsp;Hz to 100&nbsp;kHz) was carried out in an electrochemical workstation (V89123, IVIUM Co., Ltd). Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (t<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) was tested in another electrochemical workstation (PARSTAT MC, AMETEK Co., Ltd.) with a combination of twice EIS and a direct current&nbsp;polarization between them. The t<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> was calculated by the Bruce–Vincent equation:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202315309-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f23aedc5-eb53-43ff-9966-a56def82a6be/adfm202315309-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202315309:adfm202315309-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202315309-math-0002.png">

                                    </mrow>
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
                                          </msub>

                                          <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">

                                                   <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                                </mrow>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi>

                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfenced>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi>

                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{\mathrm{t}}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{\mathrm{ + }}}{\mathrm{\ = }}\frac{{{{\mathrm{I}}}_{\mathrm{s}}\ \left( {\Delta {\mathrm{V\ -\ }}{{\mathrm{I}}}_{\mathrm{0}}\ {{\mathrm{R}}}_{\mathrm{0}}} \right)}}{{{{\mathrm{I}}}_{{\mathrm{0\ }}}\left( {\Delta {\mathrm{V\ }} - {\mathrm{\ I}}_{\mathrm{s}}{\mathrm{\ R}}_{\mathrm{s}}} \right)}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  
                  <p>To explore the practicability of prepared anodes, full cells were assembled using obtained samples as anodes and NVP as cathodes with 50&nbsp;µL of 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DIGLYME as the electrolyte and Polypropylene as separator. The performance of both symmetric cells and full cells was tested in a LAND battery test system (CT2001A), while the cycling stability of symmetric cells was tested in different surface current densities, and cycling stability of full cells was tested between 2.5 and 3.8&nbsp;V at 30&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>

                  <li data-bib-id="adfm202315309-bib-0001"><span class="bullet">1</span>a) <span class="author">D. Kundu</span>, <span class="author">E. Talaie</span>, <span class="author">V.
